Orville Schell: The View From the Front Row of the Trump-Xi Summit | The Foreign Affairs Interview

Foreign Affairs(126.0K) ·41:26 ·19.0K views · 2026-05-16
Orville Schell (Expert on U.S.-Chinese relations) Donald Trump (President of the United States) Xi Jinping (President of China)
The Trump-Xi summit in Beijing prioritized personal rapport and "strongman" optics over substantive policy breakthroughs, signaling a potential shift toward transactional "strategic stability" while leaving core tensions—such as Taiwan and industrial policy—largely unresolved.
TL;DR The Trump-Xi summit in Beijing prioritized personal rapport and "strongman" optics over substantive policy breakthroughs, signaling a potential shift toward transactional "strategic stability" while leaving core tensions—such as Taiwan and industrial policy—largely...

TL;DR

The Trump-Xi summit in Beijing prioritized personal rapport and "strongman" optics over substantive policy breakthroughs, signaling a potential shift toward transactional "strategic stability" while leaving core tensions—such as Taiwan and industrial policy—largely unresolved.

The Personal Dynamic: "Strongman" Rapport

  • Mutual Craving for Respect: Orville Schell notes that both Donald Trump and Xi Jinping share a desire for expressions of admiration and "fawning." Trump’s effusive respect for Xi worked a certain "magic," satisfying the Chinese government's long-standing demand for "mutual respect" that previous U.S. administrations withheld due to human rights and trade concerns.
  • The "Old Friend" Persona: Trump leaned heavily into his history with Xi, branding himself as the American president who has known the Chinese leader longest. Schell describes this approach as a "transactional and somewhat synthetic" attempt to build a personal bond that transcends traditional diplomacy.
  • A Shift in Administration Tone: Unlike Trump’s first term, which was dominated by China hawks, this visit showcased a "reborn" delegation. Even traditionally hawkish figures like Secretary of State Marco Rubio appeared to adopt the President's deal-making posture, softening their anti-communist rhetoric for the duration of the summit.

Trade and the "CEO SWAT Team"

  • Business-First Diplomacy: Trump arrived with a high-profile "SWAT team" of tech and finance leaders, including Elon Musk and Jensen Huang (NVIDIA), to signal American economic clout.
  • Transactional Wins: The summit produced "optics-heavy" commercial promises, including:
      • Agricultural Commodities: Commitments for China to purchase billions of dollars in U.S. soybeans.
      • Aviation: Preliminary agreements for the purchase of 200 Boeing aircraft.
      • Institutional Framework: The proposed establishment of a joint "board of trade" to manage commercial relations.
  • The "Era of Choke Points": Schell argues that despite the "bonhomie," the relationship is now defined by mutual leverage. China holds the advantage in critical minerals and rare earths, while the U.S. maintains a lead in microchips and fabrication technology.

Geopolitical Flashpoints: Taiwan and Iran

  • Taiwan as the "Home Wrecker": Xi Jinping prioritized the Taiwan issue early, warning that it could lead to conflict if mishandled. However, both sides essentially "kicked the can down the road" to preserve the summit’s positive atmosphere.
  • Strategic Ambiguity: Trump appeared to maintain "strategic ambiguity" regarding Taiwan. Schell suggests Trump may "slow-walk" approved arms sales to Taiwan as a bargaining chip for trade concessions, such as agricultural purchases.
  • Vague Promises on Iran: While Trump claimed Xi promised to help secure the Strait of Hormuz, Schell remains skeptical, noting that China’s deep ties with Iran make meaningful pressure unlikely.

Historical Context and Internal Pressures

  • The End of "Engagement": Schell views this era as a departure from the "Engagement" period initiated by Deng Xiaoping. He argues that Xi Jinping has abandoned "reform" as the "solvent" that once made the U.S. and China feel comfortable converging.
  • Domestic Fragility in China: Despite Xi’s outward confidence, Schell highlights significant internal pressures:
      • Elite Disaffection: Growing dissatisfaction among urban professionals and educated classes.
      • Military Purges: Ongoing anti-corruption investigations within the Central Military Commission.
  • The "Concession" Problem: For this summit to be a true inflection point, both sides would need to make real concessions. However, Schell notes that Xi Jinping views concessions as "hallmarks of weakness" and lacks the diplomatic "gene" for compromise.
"What makes Trump so interesting and hard to read... is that he himself is a kind of contradiction in which he has these sort of very hawkish tendencies, and on the other hand, he has this sort of mentality of, you know, big leader to big leader, we should be able to make a deal and work things out." — Orville Schell
"Xi Jinping did not support reform which was the sort of solvent that made the U.S. and China feel comfortable together... When that ended, then engagement ended, and then we got into this sort of much more hostile, antagonistic framework." — Orville Schell
"China’s strategy remains the following: become as autarkic and independent of choke points from Europe and the United States... but cultivate as much dependence in those countries as you possibly can because it’s a lever." — Orville Schell
I’m Dan Kurtz-Phelan and this is the Foreign Affairs Interview. I think it is true that what makes Trump so interesting and hard to read, and also sometimes hard to deal with, is that he himself is a kind of contradiction in which he has these sort of very hawkish tendencies, and on the other hand, he has this sort of mentality of, you know, big leader to big leader, we should be able to make a deal and work things out. Orville Schell may be America’s greatest chronicler and observer of several decades of U.S.-China relations. We were extremely lucky to have him in Beijing this week for the just concluded summit between Donald Trump and Xi Jinping—far from the first encounter between U.S. and Chinese leaders for which Schell has had a front-row seat. I spoke to him on Friday, May 15th, in the morning New York time and evening Beijing time about how he read the interactions between Xi and Trump, what they did and did not say about the hardest and most dangerous issues in the U.S.-China relationship, and how this could mark an inflection point for the two countries. Orville, great to talk to you on your Friday night Beijing time. Trump started his trip home a few hours ago, but I realize you’ve had an intense few days. It’s been an intense and quite interesting few days. The President took off in Air Force One a couple of hours ago, and I think the Chinese and, I think, President Trump were quite pleased with the outcome. You were of course on that, the last presidential trip to to China when Trump went in 2017. As you’ve watched Trump and Xi this time around, how does their dynamic seem, different or the same to you? How have you read the body language and symbolism and rhetoric that you’ve seen from them? Well, one of the things which was very evident and President Trump kept emphasizing it, and that was that he has now, as he says, known and been a friend of Xi Jinping since his first administration. And he likes to, and did, sort of tout the fact that he is the American president that has known Xi Jinping or the leader of China for longer than anyone else. So he kind of fancies himself, I think, on this trip, sort of becoming an old friend of Xi, someone with some standing, and was very effusive in his sort of expressions of respect and appreciation of Xi, which I think was very gratifying to him. Because as you know, the Chinese government and even Xi Jinping is often sort of uttering this sort of mantra of "we need to have mutual respect and understanding." And of course, I think in various previous administrations, because of the nature of the political system and rights violations and theft of intellectual property—one could go on—that measure of respect was not really conferred. But I think the way Trump treated Xi was, you know, quite admiring and, I would say, very respectfully, if in a somewhat transactional and some might even say somewhat synthetic way. But I think it did work its magic in a certain sense. How did you read the Chinese reception? Both the kind of preparations for Trump and then the theatrics, the choreography of the whole thing. What did that tell you about what the Chinese were trying to get out of that and whether Trump’s sense of himself as this old friend of Xi Jinping and of China was exactly what they were going for? Well, I think they sensed within Trump, unlike the first administration where, as you recall, the China hawks were much in greater predominance within the administration. This time, I think they sensed that there was a certain kind of ambiguity within him that they needed to leverage. And I think it is true that what makes Trump so interesting and hard to read, and also sometimes hard to deal with, is that he himself is a kind of contradiction, in which he has these sort of very hawkish tendencies, and on the other hand, he has this sort of mentality of, you know, big leader to big leader, we should be able to make a deal and work things out. So in the first administration, I think the more hawkish element was evident, and he was a little less comfortable and a little less confident of his ability to sort of play Xi. This time, I think, in a certain sense, he and Xi are quite similar in that both of them do really appreciate, even crave, a sense of their counterpart expressing respect, admiration, and possibly even a little fawning. So both leaders, I think, sort of connected on that level, that they both were questing after the other side’s expression of admiration and respect, and each of them got it from the other in spades on this trip. And I think it did smooth things out, and, you know, that their hope is that this will be a sort of a turning point, an inflection point. And I think that’s an interesting question, and what constitutes an inflection point? Surely not just a little bonhomie and a little expression of, you know, mutual interest in win-win, you know, the usual bromides. You’ve been on how many presidential visits to China at this point? Do you know off the top of your head? Does it go back to Carter or before Ford even? Yeah, I started when Deng Xiaoping came to extend diplomatic relations with Jimmy Carter, and that was quite an extraordinary trip. And then I’ve been on, oh, three or four others since then: an Obama trip, I went on the trip with Clinton in ‘98 when Jiang Zemin was party general secretary, the last Trump trip, and I went on a Bush senior trip in, I think it was 1986. And so I, I mean, to watch these kinds of trips evolve has been very interesting and also to take measure of who it is on each side that is interacting. And what becomes very obvious to me, particularly if you look at the Chinese side—I mean, the leaders from Hu Yaobang to Zhao Ziyang to Jiang Zemin, Hu Jintao, and now Xi Jinping, they’re very different people. And, yes, they’re all devoted to the party and devoted to the sort of changing of agendas, but we’ve had a very stern and militant and violent revolutionary period under Mao. Then we had this reform period which was completely unexpected when Mao died in ‘76 and suddenly Deng Xiaoping reappeared in ‘78 and then ‘79 and initiated this reform period and had that sort of astounding decade of the 1980s. Of course, as we all remember, that ended with the tragedy of the Tiananmen demonstrations and massacre in 1989. And yet, when Jiang Zemin, who was appointed by Deng Xiaoping, who had been the party secretary and mayor of Shanghai, came in, reform did return. And the level of sort of friendly interaction with the United States, which came to be known as engagement, did regenerate. And so the 90s was also a surprisingly open era despite the crackdown after 1989. And then came this sort of interlude with Hu Jintao, who was very hard to read; he was sort of almost like a blank slate. But you could see then even the turning towards, you know, China becoming a little bit more full of itself, casting itself around the world, throwing its weight around, and then came Xi Jinping. And that basically was the end of engagement because I think Xi Jinping did not support reform, which was the sort of solvent that made the U.S. and China feel comfortable together. As long as reform was going on, one could imagine we were converging in some manner, not rapidly and not completely, but the trend line seemed encouraging. And when that ended, then engagement ended, and then we got into this sort of much more hostile, antagonistic framework. And so I think that’s what they were trying to sort of overcome in some sense in this summit, both because Trump knows we’re in the era of choke points when China has its choke points and leverage it can pull against the United States—and one of them is critical minerals and rare earth, but many others—and we have a few that we can pull against them, namely microchips and, you know, chip fab technology. And so this has created a much more hostile and much more antagonistic sort of interaction, where the sort of proclivity of the Chinese Communist Party to see the United States again as what they call a hostile foreign force has sort of come back. So that was the backdrop against which this meeting sort of was set. Even with that shift, are there moments that you’ve thought back to on those earlier visits that either because they were so different or because you see kind of echoes or parallels? Yes, I mean, certainly, well, I mean during the ‘79, 1979 trip when Deng Xiaoping showed up in Washington, I mean, that was one of the most rapid sort of transformations of political attitude, I think I’ve observed, in the United States that suddenly the sort of very anti-communist Cold War mentality seemed to evaporate. And, you know, corporate leaders and even politicians were sort of slavishly interested in this strange sort of, you know, reconnection of the U.S. and China. And Deng Xiaoping, who was, you know, a very short man, not a man of great stature—five-foot-four, I think, inches high—but they suddenly forgot about all the Cold War antipathy and they were all crowding into the White House and the National Gallery to genuflect to him and it was a real love fest. And that sort of was the permission that both sides gave to each other, to their people, to sort of let the Cold War drift off and to try to restore a more friendly and open and constructive relationship. So I think it’s those periods that Trump and Xi sort of look back to now and want to reproduce. And then, of course, there was 1972 before 1979 when Nixon and Kissinger did that incredible turnaround with Mao Zedong and Zhou Enlai. So I think those two moments were the moments of great change and really, sort of, points of inflection. And I think Trump aspires to greatness and to being a great global leader as well as a great MAGA leader at home, and he looks to that, and he would like to help reproduce some such moment. But, as we can discuss, it’s going to be quite difficult despite the atmospherics and the optics which are very good I think at this summit, but they dodged almost every issue that sort of underlies why the U.S.-China relationship over these past decades has been so fraught. Xi Jinping put forward this kind of new framing of the relationship. The Chinese language, which I believe maybe first came up in the statements ahead of dinner on Thursday, at least publicly, was that the two sides are going to, quote, "build a constructive China-U.S. relationship of strategic stability" as the new positioning for China-U.S. relations. I guess the shorthand is "constructive strategic stability," which they were using today on Friday. Do you see anything new in this in terms of signals or symbolism, or is this just kind of new tortured boilerplate that replaces the old tortured boilerplate? Well, I think you know that that is the aspiration, isn’t it? And I think it actually is the aspiration of most American leaders and because there is a lot of common interest, but so far we’ve failed to be able to deploy that common interest—I mean, whether it’s world peace, the global marketplace, climate change, pandemics—we have not been able to rise to the occasion. And you have to ask yourself why. And so I think it is the answer to the why question: all of the things which have divided us, set us apart, whether it’s political systems, whether it’s theft of intellectual property, whether it’s dumping, industrial policy, you name it. And it was those issues which they did not, they didn’t have an answer to and they didn’t even really address in this summit. For instance, before the summit really began, Xi Jinping made a statement on Taiwan, which is of course the big home wrecker of every Sino-U.S. negotiation, and he said that this is a matter of great importance to China and if it wasn’t handled well, it could end in conflict. But the interesting thing was he said it sort of before anything happened, and then they didn’t seem to return to it. Now we don’t have the text of the exact negotiations which went behind closed doors, but there was no raising of that. So they kind of kicked that can down the road again, and, but it is still there. There’s, as you know, some twenty-five billion dollars in two packages going through Congress for arms sales to Taiwan. One’s been approved; it’s at the White House just being, you know, final details being roughed out, and there’s another one. I think we have to watch very carefully what’s going to happen to those now. Now it could be that our supply line is so bollixed up that we can’t get it to Taiwan very soon anyway, even though they paid for the first one. Maybe Trump will just quietly say to Xi, or has said to Xi, "Listen, you can count on us to slow-walk this one. Maybe I have to do the first one but we’re not going to do it in a hurry and the second one, let’s you and I keep talking and see what we can work out." So there may be things that still are owed that Trump has to pay in order to get a deal on soybeans—they’re going to buy billions of dollars of soybeans and two hundred Boeing planes and they’re going to set up a board of trade—there are many things that will emerge, I think, in a more concrete form in the days to come. But still, there are these issues like Taiwan and economic issues about dumping and balances of trade and things like that which they don’t have answers for. And I think it will be very difficult for them to carry out some major transformation of attitude with these issues sort of lying just beneath the surface and, like lava vents, sort of pulsing up from time to time and just making it very difficult. There was so much speculation and anxiety in American commentary and also in Chinese commentary before the visit that Xi would try to press Trump to change the American language on Taiwan’s independence—to say, to go from, you know, "we do not support Taiwan independence" to "we oppose Taiwan independence." It seems that the Chinese either decided that that wasn’t going to happen or perhaps they tried and didn’t get it, but that seemed notable that there was so little to that effect, which suggests that the Chinese have kind of lower ambitions there than we thought. I think that’s why Xi set it up front; he got it off the table kind of and he got a chance to let the Americans know this is no joke, this is really important to him. And I just read a tweet from Air Force One in which Trump made some expressions about Taiwan not, not, not being so important and I think you may find him playing down Taiwan but not doing anything so overt as to say we oppose independence or to change the balance. And Rubio did say that, that there is not going to be any change in the Taiwan policy here in Beijing. Interestingly, you know, Marco Rubio was once a hawk, but here he was coming to China not only as the Secretary of State, the head of the NSC Security Council, AID, and head of the Archives of Washington, you know, head of everything. And in the fact that he has in a certain way been reborn to follow the Trump line now of sort of deal-making and acting nice and not expressing his anti-communist hawk side. So, but that’s still there. I mean, in a certain sense Rubio is emblematic I think of, of the deeply embedded suspiciousness of China and the whole political system, the human rights questions, all, all of that bundle of things, and so, you know, we can’t expect that just to evaporate. So that a lot of talk about win-win and and Trump’s respect for Xi as he expressed it many, many times, will sort of overcome to transcend some of these other things; they are not going to just evaporate. I was struck also in in in Trump’s comments on Taiwan I think also in Air Force One that he seemed to get strategic ambiguity right; this was something that that Biden struggled with because Biden would come out and say say something clear and unambiguous about the American commitment to Taiwan in the event of an attack and Trump, Trump seemed to get it right in fact. You know, I have to say on this trip, Trump stuck to the script and he didn’t sort of wander off into the bog of his normal kind of, you know, just free-ranging and just saying stuff. He would do a little embroidery, but at the dinner last night at the banquet, the welcome banquet at the Great Hall of the People, he actually read this speech. He said things that, you know, he wouldn’t dream up himself about the longevity of the U.S.-China relationship and the historical fund of amity that’s been built up and things like that. So I think his expression on strategic ambiguity is, I think, they’ve decided that they don’t want to have a big fight in Congress and back in the U.S., so they’re not going to, at least immediately and overtly, give in to Xi’s demand for some radical new expression. But who knows what they’re saying in private? You know, we’ll have to see when we get that transcript, if we do. There did seem to be a kind of all hands effort both publicly and privately to get the message to Trump from people in his own administration and Congress and elsewhere not to rock the boat here, which seems to have gotten across. It was notable that Scott Bessent, the Treasury Secretary, really took the lead in setting up this trip. As I read the rhetoric on commercial ties and business ties—I mean, it’s of course correct that there wasn’t, you know, major change in rebalancing the economic relationship or anything like that—but you have these, you know, kind of much fanfare around around purchases of soybeans and agricultural products and Boeing airplanes. It did almost bring to mind the very old rhetoric about the Chinese market and the potential of business ties, whether from, you know, 1940s pre-revolution or from the 1990s. It was a kind of striking rhetorical return to eras when we talked about the business potential very differently. Yes, and I think that was front and center and, as you know, he rounded up the heads of a lot of tech, mostly tech companies and some finance companies as well. And I mean this was sort of his SWAT team and, of course, Jensen Huang of NVIDIA, I think for some reason had said he couldn’t come on the trip but he was summoned urgently at the last moment and flew his private jet to Anchorage and got in Air Force One. I think Trump wanted to sort of bring as much sort of clout as he could, particularly in the tech world, with him to China. And I think the Chinese appreciated that. So Trump felt well-armed with some business supporters, or at least not critics, and, you know, he had Elon Musk and you know the list. He wasn’t just an isolated figure on a summit trip; he was someone with this team—it wasn’t an enormously large team but it was a very impressive team in terms of the clout that they had—and were sitting there at the banquet and they had a private session with Li Qiang, the, you know, the Prime Minister, etc. The Chinese focus on Taiwan was in some ways mirrored by the American interest in getting some Chinese help on Iran. I think the hope would be that the Chinese government would put some pressure on Iran to get to a deal and reopen the Strait of Hormuz and all of that. Trump suggested that there was at least some agreement on how the two sides saw the situation or saw the need for the strait to be open. Did you pick up anything about progress on Iran, any meaningful change? Well, you know, it’s very hard to judge. I mean, Trump had an interview with Hannity. You know, they had all these tents set up at the China World Hotel where each network had a tent and there was Hannity holding court in the tent. But he went over to the Great Hall of the People to do an interview with Trump. Trump said two or three times in one little sentence that, you know, Xi Jinping has said to him he would do whatever he could to help in the Strait of Hormuz. Well, it’s easy to say that, but what does it actually mean? We don’t know. But to Trump, that was a very valuable thing to be able to say that his good friend had promised to help. But you know, China and Iran have a long relationship and the Foreign Minister of Iran was just here, and it’s hard to know exactly what those suggestions or suggested promises might mean. I don’t know. I think so far so good. They laid a good atmospheric sort of platform down where everybody’s happy. Chinese television after Trump left was just all sort of expressing great, great sort of satisfaction at how friendly it had been and how well it had gone. But it doesn’t change the reality of the issues, some of which are very, very difficult to solve and long-lived, that have deep, deep historical roots. Unlike almost, you know, most of your colleagues, this is not your first time in China or not one of your first trips. I imagine you have had conversations with Chinese journalists or Chinese friends who are reacting. Are there interesting differences or reactions you’ve seen from them that shed light on the kind of Chinese intentions or reactions here? Well, I think it’s fair to say that, you know, sort of ordinary working-class people in China, they pretty much stay out of politics and they don’t have a hostile attitude, I think, towards Xi Jinping. But professional people, educated people, urban people—that’s another story. And I think there is a lot of dissatisfaction here with the direction that Xi is taking China, and a lot of people are moving abroad. And that’s a real index, I think, of the level of disaffection. So even though China seems to be making significant progress in terms of technical development and rejuvenation and many others, possibly even the property market is showing a few signs of being stabilized, but there is a lot of dissatisfaction. And Xi Jinping knows it. And he’s got problems in the military—he’s, you know, the Central Military Commission now has one other commissioner besides Xi—all the others are being tried for corruption and this, that, and the other. Who knows what the real reason is that they’ve been "had," as the Chinese say, been "capped," had their dunce hat put on their head in effect, and they’ve lost their jobs and they’re undergoing investigation. So there are a lot of things lurking beneath the surface, both internally in China and beneath the surface, as I said before, of the U.S.-China relationship that this summit neither addressed nor, I think, would have the power to remedy. And they’re bound to keep popping up. So exactly what the long-term effect will be, that’s another question. There’s been lots of reporting and lots of reason to think the reporting is right to the fact that Xi Jinping is in a period of great confidence, and some would call it hubris, you know, and he has reason to see China’s position in a pretty good light—he’ll presumably start a fourth term next year in 2027. Do you think he’s overconfident, do you think he’s kind of overreading some of the short-term developments and that there is risk right now both for him and for the relationship in hubris rather than anything else? You know, how to describe it, whether it’s hubris or just a... I mean, it seems to me we’re in a world now where there’s a whole group of leaders—I would include Trump, Putin, Xi, Kim Jong-un, and even at least before the recent bombings in Iran, the mullahs—who have great pretensions of grandiosity to restore their countries to greatness. And in the case of Russia and China, of course, that is a kind of imperial greatness too where they not only flesh out the most expansive borders of what was Russia and what was China—including Tibet, Manchuria, Mongolia, Hong Kong, Taiwan, South China Sea—but also to restore it to soothe their wounded egos that have historically waited for this moment when China would have the wealth and power to throw its weight around and be a big power itself. And perhaps even earn the right to do a little bullying, as all great powers have. So I think that’s a very powerful impulse and of course it does raise the question of: what’s China after? Do they want global hegemony? Could they cooperate with the United States ever again? What are they going to do about the EU and Europe? In other words, what’s their version of a new world order? I’m fascinated by this idea that this could be an inflection point that you raised earlier and, notwithstanding all the hard issues that could, you know, kind of drag us back to where the relationship was several months ago, if it is an inflection point, where do you think we’re heading? What comes after after the inflection? Well, we’d have to start having some real concessions made by both sides. Now, I don’t think that’s in the cards; I don’t think Xi Jinping is ready to make major concessions. I think he views such concessions as hallmarks of weakness. I mean, we’d have to have some kind of a of a conclusion to the extravagant claims in the South China Sea. And maybe some expression of... I mean, remember what Mao Zedong said about Taiwan when Nixon and Kissinger were there. He said it’s okay if we can’t solve this for a hundred years. So in other words, he wasn’t going to make it a condition of gaining, of allowing the other sort of forms of cooperation to be impeded. And then when Deng Xiaoping went to Japan in ‘79 on his way to Washington, he was asked about Taiwan and he said, "Listen, let’s leave this for smarter generations to come to solve." Now that’s a very good formula, but I don’t think Xi Jinping is capable of going to a formula like that because it would make him look weak having sort of professed with such militance that Taiwan is ours, it’s a sensitive issue, and if we can’t get it peacefully we’ll get it through conquest or through embargo or whatever. So I just don’t see him—he could surprise us, but he doesn’t seem to have that gene which codes for giving a little to get a little, which is the essence of diplomacy. Trump is not exactly a master at this either. I mean, Biden could have done it, but when China was sort of on a roll and becoming more and more wealthy and powerful and influential and successful both at home and Belt and Road and BRICS and all of these things, they weren’t in any mood to say, "Okay, how can we get along better, what do we have to give in order to get something?" It’s notable to me that China now seems to be okay with the idea of putting guardrails on competition, really accepting that the relationship will be competitive but you need to contain it in certain ways. There was a time when you would hear from Chinese analysts that it was not in China’s interest to have guardrails around competition because it would just allow the U.S. to contain China, allow the U.S. to be more aggressive in some sense because things wouldn’t escalate. That does seem to be a meaningful change, and whether that’s a major historical inflection point, who knows, but that does seem to be a positive development. It didn’t start with this trip but this trip I think reinforced it. Yes, I think there is a greater tolerance. They used to loathe the word "competition," but now I think they’re coming to accept it much more readily. But you know what hasn’t gone away is, of course, their industrial policy. And what hasn’t gone away is the recognition—and I think they sort of learned this from us actually—that everybody needs to sort of weaponize their supply chains and needs to find choke points, that we’re in this era of choke points. What leverage do you have, not just diplomatically but economically? So tragically, I think this great global proposition which we all sort of merrily sailed into—where there was win-win, everybody profits by the global marketplace, nobody cared where stuff came from, what kind of government they had, what the geopolitics were, if you could get it cheap, fast, and didn’t have to maintain big inventories—that has changed. China’s strategy remains the following: become as autarkic and independent of choke points from Europe and the United States, Australia, Canada, Japan, etc., but cultivate as much dependence in those countries as you possibly can because it’s a lever. And so I think that’s one reason they welcomed all these American entrepreneurs there today who are all, you know, champing at the bit to get back into China. But if they do, that gives China immense leverage, just as they have over Germany now. Germany made a lot of money in the auto industry in China, and now they’re getting squeezed out by new BYDs and, you know, various other EVs and battery industry and one thing and another. Or BASF, the biggest chemical company in the world, that’s going to get squeezed out. So they were doing fine, but the problem is that it made an encumbrance on Germany, who had to trim their jibs in order to keep these companies able to keep making money in China, and it sort of distorted the geopolitical landscape. So once you’re dependent on China, you’re making money there, they sort of have a lever on you. And this is a very calculated strategy that I think Xi Jinping has masterfully concocted. Let me try to end on a less wonky note than we normally would. You’ve written so beautifully over the years about the kind of place for humanism and sort of national spirit as you’ve put it in China’s rise. You’ve kind of been skeptical that the CCP is really a party that can allow this to develop in the way that is necessary for the rise to continue. As you’ve been back this time and as you’ve read the rhetoric and symbolism of this visit, how do you see that developing? Do you see interesting changes in the national spirit as you’ve put it, do you see it being constrained, repressed? How do you read that at this moment? You know, I think what made China so interesting in the 80s, 90s, and even early 2000s was that it was both technologically and in a business sense vibrant and things were happening, but also it was a very interesting intellectual climate, a cultural climate. And, you know, publishing and music and art and philosophy, academia, think tanks—there was a lot of independent thinking and that’s where humanism sort of had an opportunity. But remember that humanism, in the sort of Marxist-Leninist playbook, is a kind of... the Chinese used to call it "spiritual pollution" because it is a universal value. The Chinese to this day do not accept the notion that there are universal values like human rights—that every human being is endowed with certain inalienable rights, you know, that kind of language. So I think the humanistic aspect is precisely, in some way, what Xi Jinping is missing. And it is sort of a tragedy because China has had such a traditionally rich culture of humanistic thinking, whether it’s religion or philosophy or art, music, you name it. And that’s sort of been exfoliated, I think, from the proposition of Chinese greatness. And in a way I think you could say the same with the United States under Trump; this isn’t his highest priority when we look at the Kennedy Center. So you have these two guys who are very transactional, and what’s important to them is trade and money and deals and calming things down so that part of the transaction can go on. They’re not so interested in in other things. For instance, there was some talk that Trump would raise the question of Jimmy Lai, who’s in for 20 years, the publisher in Hong Kong, and I think he did raise it. But I was watching television yesterday and when CNN brought up the question of Jimmy Lai and what had happened, the screen went blank. And I suspect that’s probably what happened if Trump raised it. I mean, there’s a small chance—and this is the way I would have done it—if Xi Jinping would relent on some humanistic issue like that, that you let a political prisoner who was unjustifiably arrested go. Maybe after Trump gets home one day, quietly Jimmy Lai shows up in England and Trump says, "Well thank you Mr. Xi for your demonstration of your humanism." I mean, possible? Not sure so likely because humanism is a kind of a corrosive force to the whole notion of sort of Leninist one-party rule, where the party comes first and you don’t want people distracted by things that sort of defile their primary commitment and loyalty to that goal. Well, Orville, a huge thank you for doing this conversation and for being there. We’re all extremely lucky to have you on the trip and in Beijing to help us interpret the signs and close read the rhetoric and everything else. So we will look forward to reading and hearing more once you’re back and have processed everything. But for now, thanks so much for doing this late in the evening in Beijing. Well, Dan, great pleasure and I look forward to more of these conversations. Thank you for listening. You can find the articles that we discussed on today’s show at foreignaffairs.com. This episode of the Foreign Affairs interview was produced by Rose Cohler and Kanishka Ahir. Our audio engineer is Christopher Cook. Original music is by Robin Hilton. Special thanks as well to Irina Hogan. Make sure you subscribe to the show wherever you listen to podcasts and if you like what you heard please take a minute to rate and review it. We release a new show every Thursday. Thanks again for tuning in.

Trump Leaves China With No Resolution | Balance of Power 05/15/2026

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·47:52 ·4.9K views · 2026-05-16
Donald Trump (President of the United States) Chris (US Senator)
The Trump-Xi summit in Beijing concluded without a resolution on Taiwan or significant trade breakthroughs, leaving the $14 billion weapons package for Taipei in limbo.
TL;DR The Trump-Xi summit in Beijing concluded without a resolution on Taiwan or significant trade breakthroughs, leaving the $14 billion weapons package for Taipei in limbo. Simultaneously, domestic markets suffered their worst sell-off since March as investors grapple with...

TL;DR

The Trump-Xi summit in Beijing concluded without a resolution on Taiwan or significant trade breakthroughs, leaving the $14 billion weapons package for Taipei in limbo. Simultaneously, domestic markets suffered their worst sell-off since March as investors grapple with hot inflation data, a prolonged conflict with Iran, and new ethics scrutiny regarding President Trump’s massive first-quarter stock trades.

US-China Relations: Strategic Stability Without Tangibles

  • The Taiwan Flashpoint: President Trump remained non-committal on the future of Taiwan independence and the pending $14 billion US weapons sale.
      • Breaking Precedent: Trump confirmed he discussed arm transfers in "great detail" with President Xi Jinping, a departure from decades of US policy to avoid consulting Beijing on such sales.
      • State Media Reaction: Beijing characterized Taiwan as the "number one issue" and warned of potential "clashes" if independence is pursued.
  • Underwhelming Economic Deliverables: Market expectations for major breakthroughs in agriculture and energy were largely unmet.
      • Boeing Deal: While a deal was discussed, it fell short of market expectations.
      • Tech Licensing: Speculation remains high regarding Nvidia’s H200 chips for the Chinese market, specifically following CEO Jensen Huang’s last-minute addition to the summit.
      • Institutional Commitments: Both nations agreed to establish a US-China Board of Trade and Board of Investment, though these mechanisms are not yet operational.
  • Ongoing Dialogue: President Xi has accepted an invitation to the White House for a state visit in September, signaling a commitment to "strategic stability" despite the lack of immediate policy results.

Market Volatility and Fed Leadership Transition

  • The "Warsh" Effect: Markets reacted sharply to the transition from Jay Powell to Kevin Warsh as Fed Chair.
      • Chair Pro Tem: The Federal Reserve announced Jay Powell will serve as chair pro tem until Warsh is officially sworn in.
      • Interest Rate Outlook: Hot inflation prints and oil prices climbing above $100/barrel have led investors to price in "higher for longer" rates, with some analysts now expecting no rate cuts for the remainder of 2026.
  • Market Sell-off: The S&P 500 suffered its worst day since March, and the Nasdaq 100 fell 1.5% as a "risk-off" sentiment took hold.
      • Yield Spikes: The 10-year Treasury yield rose to 4.6%, while the 2-year yield climbed above 4.1%.
      • Semiconductor Slump: The Philadelphia Semiconductor Index dropped nearly 4%, with significant losses for Intel, Micron, and Nvidia.

Ethical Scrutiny and Domestic Political Battles

  • Presidential Stock Trades: Fresh scrutiny has emerged over 3,700 stock trades made by President Trump in Q1, involving companies with significant business before his administration.
      • Conflict of Interest Concerns: Specific trades in Nvidia, Microsoft, and Amazon occurred shortly before major policy announcements or government contracts involving those firms.
  • Redistricting and Midterm Implications: GOP-led redistricting efforts are intensifying ahead of the November elections.
      • South Carolina: A special session aims to eliminate the minority-majority district currently held by Democratic Representative Jim Clyburn.
      • Louisiana Primary: Senator Bill Cassidy faces a difficult path to a runoff as the primary has become a referendum on his standing with Trump-aligned voters.
  • Defense Readiness: Becca Wasser (Bloomberg Economics) warned that the ongoing conflict with Iran is depleting US munitions reserves, potentially hindering the military's ability to defend Taiwan in a near-term conflict.

Notable Quotes

"On Taiwan he feels very strongly. I made no commitment either way."

— President Donald Trump (on his conversation with President Xi)

"My core concern all along has been that Trump would trade chips for beans—more purchases of soybeans by China in exchange for licensing more advanced AI chips."

— Senator Chris Coons (D-DE)

"The [Fed] chair's legacy is really going to be around preserving the independence of the Federal Reserve, and I think he actually gives Kevin Warsh a much stronger position to start from than might have been."

— Lael Brainard (Former Vice Chair of the Federal Reserve)

"It’s nuts that people in office are able to—and it’s not really just the president, it’s members of Congress—trade on maybe not inside information, but clearly advanced views of what’s going on."

— Christopher Smart (Founder, Arbroath Group)

Thank you for being with us on Balance of Power. alongside Kailey Leinz, I'm Joe Mathieu on Bloomberg TV and radio. “The integrity with which he led this institution and really protected its independence during a time when the president mounted a series of unprecedented actions—firing a governor, undertaking a criminal investigation. He's also former Special Assistant to the president on the National Economic Council in the Obama administration. Christopher, it's great to see you. Welcome back. You know the landscape here. Well, that's a trick question if I ever heard one on a Friday afternoon. I think the markets are clearly looking at all of the pressures coming from energy prices. Now the markets are pricing in nothing this year and maybe not even next year at this stage. There are a lot of moving parts as any central banker would tell you. In that central banker-ese that they quickly adopt when they get into office, they talk about the data or still looking to see what's coming into view. For any central banker, the best answer they can give is that right now we're going to do nothing. The shocks from oil price spikes really are meant to be temporary. What really is concerning and what I think they're all going to have their eyes on are things around inflation expectations which have been edging up, stability in bond markets, and then the data in terms of what kind of weakness we may see in consumer spending which has been holding up pretty well. So what is going on in the bond market, Christopher? Why the freakout today? This is something that could have taken place at the beginning of strikes against Iran, but two months have gone by and now suddenly today you've got a two-year above 4%, a 10-year above 4.5%, and a stock market that can't handle it. Well, that's the mystery of markets. The CPI print earlier this week was certainly not a surprise. The continuing standoff between Iran and the US with no apparent way out is not a surprise. The fact that Xi Jinping couldn't or wouldn't—to my mind it's really hard to imagine that he really can pressure Iran to change its course any better than the United States can—that's not a surprise either. These are things that happen in markets, right? They all sort of accumulate over a period of weeks and days and then all of a sudden people kind of look at each other and go, "Ugh, this is really bad." I think that's what we're seeing on a Friday afternoon. What was your overall reading of the President's trip to Beijing? How should investors think about it when it seems that the success was really in the fact that there was dialogue and the trade detente seems to be intact? Well, I think it's plenty when you think back to where we were last fall or even last year where we were mounting tariffs of 100 or 125% on one another. We had actually found each other's most sensitive pressure points with the Chinese cutting off access to critical minerals and the Americans cutting off China's access to our most advanced semiconductors. Then both sides kind of backed away from that. I think the fact that this was a good summit symbolically—the diplomatic equivalent of the Hippocratic Oath to do no harm—is key. The fact that the two leaders want to play up the fact that they had a good personal relationship is all good. It's not going to solve the underlying structural issues that divide the two countries. But I think it also gives us now a scorecard between now and the next 129 days, which is when Xi Jinping is due to land in Washington for a bilateral summit. 🔴 DIRECTO VISIÓN GLOBAL | Trump abandona China sin grandes acuerdos y Warsh se estrena en la FED

Intereconomía News(439.0K) ·61:26 ·184 views · 2026-05-16
Donald Trump (President of the United States) Xi Jinping (President of China) Kevin Warsh (Official, Federal Reserve)
Donald Trump’s historic visit to Beijing concluded with modest commercial gains (Boeing, Nvidia) and a fragile diplomatic truce, while Kevin Warsh succeeds Jerome Powell as Fed Chair amid stagflationary pressures and low consumer confidence.
TL;DR Donald Trump’s historic visit to Beijing concluded with modest commercial gains (Boeing, Nvidia) and a fragile diplomatic truce, while Kevin Warsh succeeds Jerome Powell as Fed Chair amid stagflationary pressures and low consumer confidence. Limited Economic Outcomes:...

TL;DR

Donald Trump’s historic visit to Beijing concluded with modest commercial gains (Boeing, Nvidia) and a fragile diplomatic truce, while Kevin Warsh succeeds Jerome Powell as Fed Chair amid stagflationary pressures and low consumer confidence.

The Trump-Xi Summit: Strategy Over Substance

  • Limited Economic Outcomes: Despite a high-profile delegation including Elon Musk and Jensen Huang, the summit yielded few major breakthroughs.
      • Key Agreements: China committed to purchasing 200 Boeing aircraft and secured renewed access to Nvidia’s H200 chips and U.S. beef plants.
      • Market Skepticism: Boeing shares fell following the announcement, as investors viewed the results as underwhelming compared to expectations.
  • The "Thucydides Trap" and Taiwan: Xi Jinping explicitly warned against a direct conflict between a rising power (China) and an established one (U.S.).
      • The Red Line: China designated Taiwan as an absolute "red line"; Trump remained non-committal on future arms sales to the island, stating he would "take decisions" later.
  • Geopolitical Philosophy: Analyst Miguel Ángel Melián noted a clash of strategic styles: the U.S. views geopolitics as Chess (defeating the adversary), while China views it as Go (occupying strategic spaces to encircle the enemy).
  • Future Outlook: Expert Rafael Galán Perpe described the summit as a "first step" toward a potential long-term framework. Xi Jinping has accepted an invitation to visit Washington this autumn.

The Federal Reserve: The Warsh Era Begins

  • Leadership Transition: On Friday, May 15, Kevin Warsh succeeded Jerome Powell as Chairman of the Federal Reserve.
  • The "Hawkish" Dilemma: Warsh, known for a less accommodative stance, faces immediate pressure from the White House to cut interest rates to stimulate growth.
  • Economic Headwinds:
      • Inflation vs. Wages: April data shows inflation at 3.8%, outpacing wage growth of 3.6%, leading to a visible loss of purchasing power.
      • Consumer Contraction: Confidence is at record lows; families are prioritizing essential goods over large purchases like cars or appliances.
  • Institutional Independence: Analysts Alexis Ortega and Marc Ribes suggest Warsh is trapped between "Main Street" (the real economy) and "Wall Street," with his primary challenge being the preservation of the Fed's political autonomy.

Geopolitical Standoffs: Iran and the Strait of Hormuz

  • Nuclear Uncertainty: Negotiations regarding Iran’s highly enriched uranium are stalled; the exact location of these reserves remains unknown following 2025 military strikes.
  • Regional Instability:
      • BRICS Failure: The latest BRICS summit in New Delhi concluded without a joint declaration due to internal tensions regarding the Middle East.
      • Shipping Risks: Iran maintains an ambiguous stance on the Strait of Hormuz, using it as a "geopolitical pressure tool" while claiming it remains open to non-belligerent nations.

Market Watch: Semiconductors and Corporate Highlights

  • Tech Sector Volatility: Wall Street saw significant declines in semiconductors following the summit. Nvidia, ARM (-6.8%), and AMD (-3.4%) fell as investors digested the lack of broader trade concessions.
  • Corporate Moves:
      • Microsoft: Shares rose 4.4% after Bill Ackman’s Pershing Square revealed a significant "highly attractive" stake in the company.
      • Applied Materials: Reported strong Q2 revenue of $7.91 billion (up 11% year-on-year), beating analyst estimates.
      • TSMC: Announced plans to reduce its stake in Vanguard International Semiconductor to 19% to focus on core business resources.
      • Magnum/Unilever: Shares in the ice cream division surged 11% on news that Blackstone and CVC are considering a buyout offer.

Notable Quotes

"I’ve talked about Taiwan and the sale of weapons... I will take decisions, but the last thing we need right now is a war 9,500 miles away. We are doing very well." — Donald Trump, President of the United States.
"The U.S. understands the world as a chessboard—you have to overthrow and diminish your adversary. China understands it as a Go board—occupying strategic spaces to progressively corner the enemy." — Miguel Ángel Melián, International Analyst.
"If Xi Jinping visits the United States [in autumn], it will be the best news for peace in recent months. If not, we will remain on edge." — Felipe Debasa, Director of the Master in EU-China, URJC.
"Warsh is the choice of the person [Trump] who pressured Powell the most... he faces a hidden inflation problem that is a supply issue, not a demand issue." — Alexis Ortega, Professor at EAE Business School.

Esa cumbre en Pekín que se salda, podríamos decir, sin grandes titulares, al menos no grandes acuerdos económicos o comerciales. Las expectativas estaban muy elevadas al respecto, y no es para menos, porque Donald Trump iba acompañado de toda una comitiva de pesos pesados, grandes magnates representantes de Wall Street y de Silicon Valley, la élite financiera y tecnológica de Estados Unidos. No ha habido grandes anuncios más allá de una compra por parte de China de 200 Boeing, el acceso que podrá tener China o algunas de sus compañías al menos a chips H200 de Nvidia, y la reapertura del acceso de China a las plantas estadounidenses de carne de vacuno. Xi Jinping habló el primer día de la trampa de Tucídides, una expresión geopolítica que viene a señalar lo que estamos viendo ahora: cómo una potencia en auge, en este caso China, puede llegar a enfrentarse a la potencia hegemónica establecida, como es el caso de Estados Unidos. Xi Jinping hablaba de evitar esta trampa de Tucídides, es decir, de evitar un conflicto directo, pero ponía una línea roja muy clara, señalaba cuál podía ser el punto que desatara ese conflicto directo: Taiwán. Donald Trump, en su vuelta en el Air Force One, dice que todavía no ha estudiado ni ha decidido nada sobre la venta de armas por parte de Estados Unidos a Taiwán. Por otro lado, seguimos con una prórroga de la tregua entre Estados Unidos e Irán que no muestra ningún avance. Y, por otro lado, hoy 15 de mayo Jerome Powell sale de la Fed como presidente y entra Kevin Warsh. Comienza una nueva era. Visión Global con Marta Isern. Radio Intereconomía. Donald Trump y Xi Jinping han cerrado en Pekín una de las cumbres más delicadas de los últimos años. El resumen podría ser diplomacia encorsetada, muchas promesas y una gran advertencia de China sobre Taiwán. Ha sido el primer encuentro en Pekín entre ambos líderes en casi una década. Donald Trump no visitaba a Xi desde su primer mandato en 2017. El presidente estadounidense ha hecho gala de una moderación poco usual en él, para qué nos vamos a engañar, pero que encaja muy bien con el estilo de su homólogo chino. Xi Jinping ha sorprendido con su lenguaje y su definición de las relaciones entre China y Estados Unidos. Ha hablado de estabilidad estratégica constructiva, que está muy ligado a su mención a la trampa de Tucídides. Viene a decir que la competencia entre ambos países es inevitable, pero busca situarla en un equilibrio. Miguel Ángel Melián, analista internacional y consultor de asuntos públicos, señala las diferencias entre dos visiones geopolíticas totalmente opuestas. Estados Unidos entiende el mundo como un tablero de ajedrez, es decir, hay que derrocar, llegar al final del tablero y conseguir mermar a tu adversario en sus múltiples capacidades. Y China lo entiende totalmente diferente: lo entiende como un tablero del Go, que básicamente es ocupar espacios estratégicos para ir acorralando progresivamente a tu enemigo. Y eso lo hemos visto; cómo China ha ido controlando los espacios comerciales, económicos y diplomáticos que Estados Unidos no ha sabido ni entender ni profundizar ni seguir desarrollando. Bueno, el Go es el arte milenario de la estrategia. Mientras Estados Unidos juega al ajedrez, China juega al Go. Tanto Trump como Xi Jinping han expresado un deseo compartido de estabilidad, pero detrás de esa aparente sintonía, los resultados concretos de la cumbre son más bien limitados. De hecho, hay quienes apuntan a una cumbre decepcionante. Los grandes anuncios se han concentrado en la compra china de 200 aviones Boeing, sin ningún tipo de celebración en los mercados. Todo lo contrario: al conocer esta noticia, Boeing caía. Además, hay avances también para que Nvidia vuelva a vender sus chips H200 en China. Cifras relevantes, sí, pero lejos de las expectativas teniendo en cuenta la presencia de grandes CEOs estadounidenses, entre ellos Elon Musk o Jensen Huang, el CEO de Nvidia. Bueno, aun así el mercado interpreta que esta cumbre no buscaba tanto cerrar grandes acuerdos inmediatos sino más bien establecer un marco de convivencia entre las dos mayores potencias del mundo. Se prevé que esta cumbre sea solo, por tanto, un primer paso para todo lo que podría seguir a continuación. Eso incluiría una posible extensión de la tregua comercial, futuras rebajas arancelarias, flexibilización de controles sobre chips y tierras raras o una mayor apertura del mercado chino a empresas estadounidenses. Por ahí pasaba también la promesa de China: una China más abierta a ese capital extranjero que necesita, por cierto. Además, también habrá una próxima cumbre. Las expectativas ya empiezan a dirigirse hacia allí porque Xi Jinping ha aceptado la invitación de Trump para visitar Estados Unidos este otoño. El gran tema ha sido Taiwán. Xi Jinping ha expuesto su gran línea roja; lo hacía ya el primer día de cumbre, y Trump ha evitado aclarar si mantendrá la venta de armas a la isla. He hablado de Taiwán, dice Trump a bordo del Air Force One, y de la venta de armas. Se ha hablado de muchos detalles, tomaré decisiones, pero lo último que necesitamos ahora mismo es una guerra a 9.500 millas de distancia. Nos está yendo muy bien. Bueno, la gran conclusión de la cumbre es que Estados Unidos e China han decidido congelar temporalmente una escalada mientras ambos ganan tiempo. Trump necesita estabilidad económica antes de las elecciones de medio mandato y Xi necesita consolidar la posición tecnológica y estratégica de China. Pero bajo la cordialidad, los banquetes y las promesas de nuevas reuniones, la rivalidad estructural entre las dos mayores potencias económicas del mundo sigue intacta. Y Taiwán, recordemos, es una línea roja capaz de hacerlo saltar todo por los aires. Estados Unidos e Irán han decidido aparcar, por ahora, uno de los asuntos más explosivos de sus negociaciones: el destino de las reservas de uranio iraní altamente enriquecido. El problema es enorme: nadie sabe exactamente dónde se encuentra desde los ataques de Estados Unidos e Israel en junio de 2025, y ahora ambas partes admiten que no están cerca de resolverlo. El ministro de Exteriores iraní, Abbas Araghchi, ha comunicado que ambos países han optado por dejar la cuestión nuclear para las últimas fases de una hipotética negociación de paz, que no está teniendo lugar. Lo ha dicho en Nueva Delhi, en el marco de una cumbre de los BRICS que, por cierto, ayer la mencionábamos, hoy ha finalizado sin una declaración conjunta debido a las tensiones en Oriente Medio. Donald Trump también ha rebajado las expectativas. Y también a bordo del Air Force One, volviendo de Pekín hacia Washington, ha dicho que Estados Unidos está dispuesto a enviar fuerzas para extraer el uranio iraní en el momento oportuno. Bueno, la frase deja claro que no existe ninguna operación inmediata ni tampoco un acuerdo cerrado. Las conversaciones para consolidar el alto el fuego siguen estancadas. El programa nuclear iraní, la reapertura del estrecho de Ormuz y el control del tráfico marítimo en el Golfo se mantienen como líneas rojas. Irán mantiene una posición ambigua sobre el estrecho porque asegura que todos los barcos pueden cruzarlo, salvo los países que estén en guerra con la República Islámica. A su vez, las autoridades iraníes insisten en coordinar el tráfico marítimo y reivindican su papel como protectores de la seguridad de Ormuz. Es decir, el régimen no quiere cerrar completamente la ruta, pero tampoco quiere renunciar a utilizarla como herramienta de presión geopolítica. Mientras tanto, las negociaciones entre Irán y Estados Unidos mediadas por Pakistán permanecen congeladas después de que Estados Unidos e Irán hayan rechazado mutuamente sus últimas propuestas. El mercado ve el estrecho de Ormuz como una bomba geopolítica activa y se pregunta cuánto tiempo puede soportar la economía mundial un Golfo, un Oriente Medio, que funciona bajo amenaza permanente. Kevin Warsh aterriza este viernes 15 de mayo en la Reserva Federal como su nuevo presidente. Y lo hace en uno de los momentos más delicados desde la pandemia, justo cuando la guerra con Irán, el choque energético y el deterioro del consumo comienzan a tensionar seriamente la economía de Estados Unidos. Donald Trump coloca a un hombre de su máxima confianza al frente del banco central. Hawkish, por cierto, un halcón, el menos acomodaticio también hay que decirlo de todos los candidatos que barajaba. El problema para Warsh es que la Casa Blanca espera una rápida bajada de tipos de interés para sostener el crecimiento, pero los datos empiezan a apuntar hacia la dirección contraria. Los últimos indicadores económicos reflejan un deterioro cada vez más visible del consumo. La confianza del consumidor está en su nivel más bajo y las encuestas reflejan el descontento de los estadounidenses por la guerra de Irán, que ya están notando en sus bolsillos. Grandes compañías ya alertan de una fuerte contracción del gasto. Las familias están aplazando compras importantes, como coches o electrodomésticos, grandes gastos, y concentran ese gasto en productos básicos, en lo que se necesita para el día a día. La sensación de pérdida de poder adquisitivo vuelve a instalarse, por tanto, en Estados Unidos. Mientras los salarios crecen un 3,6% interanual, es la cifra de abril, la inflación avanza un 3,8%. Sí, dos décimas que creo que se notan mucho. Los precios vuelven a subir más rápido que los ingresos de los trabajadores, la inflación sigue viva y la Fed no puede repetir el error de 2021 y señalar una inflación transitoria. El analista y profesor de EAE Business School, Alexis Ortega, enumera los tres grandes hándicaps de Kevin Warsh. Ha sido el elegido por la persona que más ha presionado a Powell y, por lo tanto, podría quedarle algún tipo de rémora de que podría ser más acomodaticio. Una inflación que no es de demanda sino que es de oferta y un problema de inflación soterrado muy importante. Por lo tanto, son tres hándicaps muy importantes o tres retos que ahora mismo va a tener sobre la mesa Kevin Warsh y esperemos que sepa solucionarlo porque, si no, nos vamos a encontrar con un problema muy grave y de una envergadura muchísimo mayor casi que la del año 2021. Este escenario deja a Warsh atrapado entre dos fuegos desde el primer día. Trump quiere tipos más bajos para estimular la economía, pero bajar tipos con una inflación todavía enquistada podría agravar más el problema de precios y erosionar la credibilidad de la Fed. Warsh llega además con un perfil muy distinto al de Jerome Powell. Es abogado de formación y tiene experiencia tanto en Wall Street como en la Casa Blanca. Ha construido su reputación por su capacidad técnica y por sus habilidades políticas y diplomáticas. Es perfectamente consciente de que la batalla por la independencia de la Fed será uno de los grandes frentes de esta nueva etapa. Marc Ribes, cofundador de Blackbird: "Y yo creo que veremos un Kevin Warsh por un lado que va a ser guardián del tamaño de balance de la Reserva Federal, pero que por el otro lado tiene que ser un poco Main Street." A lo mejor ahí lo que encaja es ver si mantiene la política monetaria o baja un poco tipos de interés para contentar a unos mientras, pues, deja de ayudar a Wall Street, al mercado especulativo, o las políticas monetarias convencionales que consisten, al final, en comprar activos en el mercado. Main Street versus Wall Street. Veremos hacia dónde se decanta Kevin Warsh. Powell será recordado por la respuesta económica frente a la crisis de la pandemia, un movimiento recordemos en dos fases: primero reducción de tipos a cero e inyección histórica de liquidez y, después, endurecimiento con agresivas subidas de tipos para frenar la inflación post-Covid. Pero entre bastidores libró otra batalla menos visible: la de reforzar los vínculos de la Fed con el Congreso para proteger la autonomía del banco central. Ni siquiera así logró escapar completamente de la polarización política. Le han seguido acusando de actuar de forma hiperpolarizada, de perjudicar deliberadamente a la economía estadounidense. Pero para muchos dentro y fuera de la Fed, Powell deja una institución más protegida políticamente de lo que estaba cuando llegó. Christine Lagarde se ha despedido públicamente de su colega y homólogo estadounidense. La presidenta del Banco Central Europeo le ha descrito como un gran líder, dedicado y comprometido. Un ejemplo a seguir y un referente para muchos. Hoy Powell deja la presidencia de la Reserva Federal y con su marcha pone fin a una era. La de Kevin Warsh acaba de empezar y ya veremos qué nos depara. Visión Global, lunes a viernes 19 a 21. Radio Intereconomía. Stripe's John Collison on How Agentic Commerce Will Reshape the Internet | Odd Lots

Bloomberg Podcasts(484.0K) ·49:35 ·1.4K views · 2026-05-16
John Collison (Co-Founder, Stripe) Joe Weisenthal (Host, Bloomberg Odd Lots) Tracy Alloway (Host, Bloomberg Odd Lots)
Agentic commerce represents the transition from manual online transactions to AI agents researching and purchasing on behalf of humans, promising a massive reduction in "friction" and a significant surge in new business formation and economic dynamism.
TL;DR Agentic commerce represents the transition from manual online transactions to AI agents researching and purchasing on behalf of humans, promising a massive reduction in "friction" and a significant surge in new business formation and economic dynamism. John Collison...

TL;DR

Agentic commerce represents the transition from manual online transactions to AI agents researching and purchasing on behalf of humans, promising a massive reduction in "friction" and a significant surge in new business formation and economic dynamism.

The Definition and Mechanics of Agentic Commerce

John Collison defines "agentic commerce" simply as AI buying things for you, split into two primary categories:

  • Consumer-Facing Commerce: AI handles the "research-to-purchase" pipeline. This includes everything from finding a niche bike case to booking a recipe’s ingredients.
  • B2B and Developer Commerce: Agents handle technical transactions autonomously.
      • The Cloudflare Example: A developer can now use an agent to register, buy, and host a domain name via Stripe’s agreement with Cloudflare, bypassing manual DNS configurations and form-filling.
  • The Friction Reduction Path: The history of payments is the elimination of friction (Credit Cards → PayPal → Apple Pay → Agents).
      • Agentic Checkout: Stripe is working with OpenAI, Google, Microsoft, and Meta to build the "wiring" that allows agents to securely use saved credentials without "flinging" credit card data across the web.

Reshaping Discovery and Consumer Behavior

The shift toward agents changes how products are found and marketed, moving away from legacy "keyword search" which Collison describes as "ridiculous" for the modern era.

  • The End of Keyword Search: AI allows for constraint-based searches (e.g., "Find a furniture piece exactly this wide") that traditional search boxes cannot handle.
  • Surfacing Niche Brands: Agents are proving to be "tasteful" and effective at finding high-quality, smaller merchants that are often buried by traditional SEO aggregators.
  • Human-in-the-Loop: For high-stakes or high-enjoyment purchases (e.g., fashion or vacation planning), humans will remain the final decider. AI serves as the researcher and executive assistant rather than a total replacement.
  • Optimizing for Agents: Retailers must move toward "machine-readable" catalogs. This requires providing real-time data on stock (SKUs) and pricing so agents don't rely on outdated training data.

The "End of Free Roaming" and Micro-transactions

A major shift in internet economics is expected as AI agents change the value of data and the viability of advertising.

  • The Pay-by-the-Sip Model: Micro-transactions, which failed for humans due to "mental load," are viable for agents.
      • Example: An agent could pay 40 cents for a specific Bloomberg data request or a few cents for a single API call without the human needing to sign up for a full subscription.
  • Stablecoins in Commerce: Collison highlights stablecoins as a potential solution for low-cost B2B transactions, allowing agents to pay for resources (like server time) instantly without traditional banking relationships.
  • Impact on Advertising: While agentic research might hurt "crummy aggregator pages" (e.g., top-10 listicles with heavy ads), Collison believes brand advertising will persist because human preference still drives the final decision in most commerce.

Economic Dynamism and Internal AI Adoption

Collison notes that the "intelligence explosion" is already manifesting in Stripe’s internal and external data.

  • Surge in Entrepreneurship: New business creation on Stripe in Q1 2025 was up 71% year-over-year. The barrier to starting a company is lower because "intelligence" (via AI) is now a recruitable resource.
  • Internal Productivity at Stripe:
      • Software Engineering: High adoption. Many bugs at Stripe are now fixed via a "Fix this bug" button where an agent writes and commits the code autonomously.
      • Sales/GTM: Highly measurable productivity gains, though specific causality is difficult to isolate.
      • Legal/Finance: Harder to automate due to the "innumeracy" of current LLMs; they can build beautiful spreadsheets where the numbers are fundamentally wrong.
  • The "TSA Pre for Bots": Stripe is developing protocols to identify "good bots" to ensure they aren't blocked by traditional anti-bot security measures while attempting to make purchases.

Notable Quotes

"It’s ridiculous that we got to the year 2026 relying on keyword search... that’s not how anyone shops in the real world." — John Collison
"A lot of websites have historically—bots used to be a bad thing. They used to discriminate against bots... whereas now it’s like, okay, no, we actually want agents to be able to do this." — John Collison
"The AIs are just uneven in which domains they are good at... they can build a really nice spreadsheet but the numbers just like go completely wrong halfway through." — John Collison
"Maybe you’re right that the really crummy aggregator pages... maybe those sites have a harder time, but I don't think anyone’s going to really be too sad about that." — John Collison

No, I—I think we’re quite skeptical of that for two reasons. One, again, with agentic commerce, we don’t think the human goes away and the human-in-the-loop goes away. And there I’d say it’s two things. One is you need a machine-compatible way to get the very latest information about the product details.
