On this question, you know, you talk about micro-transactions and people have been talking about micro-transactions for literally decades probably, and the example you gave, 'oh, why can’t—we just buy an article for 50 cents,' I think there’s various reasons why that never took off. But one aspect is just the cost of a transaction. And with—theoretically—particularly with like stablecoins, you could have almost free transactions. Are you seeing actual use of stablecoins in sort of B2B transactions because the sheer cost is so low? Like where do stablecoins come into this? You're right that when you have this pay-by-the-sip model, you obviously just can’t economically speaking have kind of an—an individual card transaction per—per usage. And by the way, this is as you know the—this is the economic model of the AI economy because you have marginal costs for each token. And so no one really offers an all-you-can-eat plan. There’s lots of, you know, you can subscribe to ChatGPT or Claude or something like that and you’ll have a plan and you’ll eventually hit usage limits. Or you can pay on an API basis and you know you’ll top up with some amount of credits and then burn down the tokens. But everyone charges per usage at some level. And so then what do you do? And the kind of common mode which we power a lot is kind of this usage-based billing—we actually bought a company called Metronome to—to power that—where you—you track credits, right? And so that’s like kind of telco billing: you have your card on file and then you track credits. We are quite excited about what you’re referencing, which is stablecoins for this. You’re asking kind of how much are we seeing of it right now. Not that much yet, but we’re building out all the infrastructure for it. The place where I think stablecoins could be really exciting is you don’t have to then go have a relationship with all these services. And so if you want to vibe code a little website and you’re like, okay, I’m going to buy the domain from Cloudflare and host it on Vercel and all these things, you then, you know, you’ve had the experience of you have to go and sign up for all these services and do these things. And we think that experience of signing up for all these services should go away. And so we are making it where you don’t need to do that and paying via stablecoins could be one way where you pay per web request or you know per individual use, very small amounts, and you don’t have to go sign up for a whole bunch of different services. So, I realize that agentic commerce is, you know, a spectrum of services, everything from just simply filling out a form to delegating, you know, giving an agent like a certain amount of wallet spend and saying, 'go off and figure out my life for me and spend the following on whatever.' Everyone also likes to talk about bots going wild, right? And going rogue. And so I imagine if you are giving an agent a certain amount of money and telling it to spend in a certain way—instruction specificity can vary—but what happens if things go bad? Or what happens if you’re simply dissatisfied with the choice that the agent makes? Who’s fault is that? Like where does the liability actually lie? I think this concern is quite overstated because one, the agents can do a huge amount of useful work for you while there’s still a human in the loop. And so, imagine if Claude code asks you 'are you okay if I spend you know this amount?' and you just have to you know hit Enter. That’s much better than the experience of signing up for a service and clicking around the web phase and things like that, so you’ve still saved a huge amount of time even if you’re kind of reviewing the transaction. Similarly, again in the consumer use case, the way you know we suggest people visualize agentic commerce is you still have a human-in-the-loop deciding, you know, making the final decision of both what thing I buy and am I okay spending this much money, but the AIs can still be hugely beneficial as part of your shopping journey even if you’re as a human signing off on that transaction. And then you get a bit into delegated authority. It’s kind of like, you know, we run a—at Stripe—a large company and we spend you know billions of dollars a year and there’s lots of delegated authority and you might say 'what if you have one crazy person who makes a bad spending decision?' And we occasionally have that. And you know, how do you solve it? It’s like, I don't know, you give people spending limits on how much they can spend and you whap them on the knuckles if they do something really crazy and you just solve it with kind of sensible limits and delegated authority and approval processes, and it’s kind of the same with a human—with an agent. Well, this gets me to a question I want to ask about running a big company in the age of AI. Things are changing extremely fast. And there are certain things that people were excited about last year that didn’t—fizzle out in the same way, or there were certain things that people got really excited about even in December 2025 that became the next hot thing. For Stripe specifically or for a company, how do you think about say like 'okay, here is a thing that we want to exploit, here is a moment and we want to take advantage of it and we’re going to build the thing for this moment' versus 'we want to allocate resources for anticipating what’s going to be the thing 24 months from now, explore where this is going'? How do you think about the sort of back—resource allocation between exploring and exploiting in such a fast-moving environment from the corporate standpoint? I would say you need to be very flexible because as you’re saying, you know, if your information is three months out of date or six months out of date, it’s just woefully old. And so we try to be pretty flexible. And I don't know, there’s—AI somehow brings out a desire for thought leadership in people and they want to make every very grand pronouncement— Oh yeah, everyone, yeah. Yeah. And just I don't know, like, we have more questions than answers and we’re trying to figure it out as we go through. So that’s one, is kind of having the right level of humility. The second thing is you kind of need to have similarly the right level of AI psychosis—the dose makes the poison—where you need to really believe in the capabilities of the models, but you also have to be willing to reason about the limits. And so to give you an example: really amazing at writing code and huge productivity uplift there. If you try to do anything, I don't know if you guys have tried to do anything like a financial analysis with the models, they’re just horrible at numbers. They’re—they’re innumerate. And they can kind of fool you because they can put together something that—they can build a really nice spreadsheet but the numbers just like go completely wrong halfway through the spreadsheet. Like, oh, great point, you’re absolutely right. And so you need to have kind of a—a bit of a fingertip sense for what the models are good at versus bad at. I kind of think of this in, you split up the company into the traditional R&D, Go-to-Market, and G&A, and you think about the different domains, they work quite differently. Within engineering, engineers have always been passionate about tools and better tools and any kind of a half-self-respecting engineer is very eagerly adopting AI, you don’t get too many laggards. And so we have seen a huge amount of adoption within software engineering at Stripe. That’s going really quickly. I don’t think we’re at the end state, but we’re pretty—like that machine I would say is pretty well set up to be in this self-improving loop because the people who are kind of doing the engineering day in and day out also think about how can I better automate this process, how can I use AI to—to make it work faster. And so, you know, a lot of bugs that are fixed at Stripe, no one ever opens an editor for them, you know, they just hit the 'Fix this bug' button in the interface and that’s done agentically. So that’s software engineering. On sales and kind of go-to-market, what helps you is that it is the most measurable domain inside of tech companies and companies broadly. And so you can reason about the specific productivity of a specific salesperson in a way that you can’t with a software engineer or, you know, a lawyer or something where any kind of a metric there is very subject to Goodhart’s law. Whereas with go-to-market, it’s measurable. And so we have seen at Stripe at least our sellers are super into AI, they’re adopting it very eagerly and, you know, you can’t ascribe causality in the productivity numbers, but the productivity numbers are very good. The place where I think it’s trickiest is within the other general functions. You think about legal and finance and risk and things like that. And there, there is a lot of AI happening, but it’s just tricky because one, there’s a huge amount of infrastructure work that needs to happen. It’s like, okay, we actually have to get this financial data into a form where it’s usable by the AI, and we used to keep it under lock and key and now we need to kind of load it in a way that the AI can use. So we had a big infrastructure build at Stripe to make the data usable to the AIs but also kind of keep it locked down appropriately and things like that. And then like I said, the AIs are just uneven in which domains they are good at. And so if it is a coding problem or if it can be turned into a coding problem, very good at that. The public tax code and the public legal code, they’re very good at that because it’s text that they’ve been trained on. But working with I would say specific financial data at Stripe or kind of specific internal stuff that we have, that’s just kind of harder to get good results out of. It’s not impossible and the models will improve a lot, but I’d say that is kind of the trickiest domain to get great results. Just going back to the beginning of this conversation, we talked about how the internet, you know, basically runs on commerce and if the nature of that commerce is changing, you would expect some impact on the internet. We’ve been asking you various questions about that, but I’m just going to ask you very directly: what is your sort of broad vision of the internet in the world of agentic commerce? We’re very excited for the intelligence explosion. And so in our pocket of the internet, we are seeing much more entrepreneurship happening. That shows up in the numbers. So to give you a sense: new business creation on Stripe in Q1 was up 71% year-over-year. That, you know, the business generally is not growing that quickly, it’s like the leading edge numbers are seeing much more business creation. You see this in other numbers too, so the App Store like number of app launches was quite flat for many, many years and now it’s seeing kind of similar growth to that. And so there are going to be many, many fruits of the intelligence explosion, but we get quite excited about the idea that you have more people creating companies. And maybe you get more dynamism, right? You know, we talked about some of the effects where, you know, you’ll maybe see more price competition. It’ll be if you work at a big company and you think 'hey, the way we are doing this is really silly, I could do it so much better,' the bar to you being able to quit the company and develop a, you know, competing better offering is—is now lower because you have more, you know, power available to you, you can recruit the intelligence that’s available to you. And so our prediction is that you get more economic dynamism, which we’re already seeing in our leading edge numbers, you know, we’re seeing more startup formation. But, you know, we talked about this at Stripe sessions, you know, kind of in a Coasean sense, maybe you see smaller firms and more of them because it’s easier for them to coordinate with each other. John, I have like a million more questions for you, but I’m aware of the time, so let’s just leave it there. This was such a treat. Really enjoy getting the chance to talk and would love to have you back sometime. Absolutely, would love it. Tracy, I really thought that conversation was super interesting. I just—I mean, just to start, there is a lot here. This is a big deal. I think like we—where it’s going to go, the question of whether just commerce directly and then also your question of the internet more broadly. There’s some pretty big questions and some pretty big implications from which way some of this stuff unfolds. Yeah, and I’m glad that John appreciates the—the enjoyability of scrolling. Human-in-the-loop. Yes, absolutely. I do have questions about, I guess, discoverability when it comes to agents. So, you know, everyone by now knows the example of, you go on Netflix, the first video or movie, TV series you ever watch is incredibly important because the algorithm sort of builds itself around that one choice. And if you watch something stupid, then forever and ever it’s going to like in-query has this one demographic. So there is a tendency for algos to sort of cluster among like recognizable patterns. So it becomes harder to discover new things, new products. I will say some algos obviously do it better than others. YouTube’s algorithm I find like actually really phenomenal for resurfacing—or for surfacing new things. Well, with—within LLMs itself, I find memory to be a very double-edged sword, which is on some level I do think it’s more useful when it knows a little bit about the context of the user. Yeah, but there are times when it’ll shoehorn any—like you did a query six months ago and it’ll try to bring it back around to that. I think like there’s a lot there, I think there’s a lot there with like the future of advertising, or just setting aside what is the equivalent of SEO in the LLM. We should probably do an episode from the perspective of retailers that are really having to think about how they surface within agentic commerce. Totally. So that in itself is a huge shift. If you’re a retailer and now you’re trying to target, you know, bots as well as humans. And then the overall question of what happens to the internet in this new world. Like, I tend to be very cynical about the overall direction of the internet and just assume that every new advance in technology is going to make it worse, but like you could see a future where the ability to do seamless micro-transactions basically means that like everyone starts charging for their content, for their APIs, and so you actually have to pay a fee to be on the internet. This is what I mean, like the end of free roaming. I think is a—an interesting question. I also just—personally, I find it very validating that like I’m not crazy that actually like being able to do something in a domain—like buying a domain name—is something that people—other people thought needed to be solved, you know what I’m saying? Like, so like some of these things like seem very specific that—that Stripe announced in the last couple weeks related to both the domain name question, the related to being able to—pay an API directly rather than go to a website and enter your credit card and stuff like that for credits or whatever. I found these things to be very annoying and big deals and I’m sort of glad that like I feel less crazy, like actually these are annoying things within the new contemporary landscape. Absolutely. And speaking of annoying things, I also feel like a big chunk of the future is going to be bots just like negotiating with each other. And I’ve already heard in the insurance sphere, you know, you’ve heard of insurers using AI to like bulk deny claims, right? And then I’ve heard of new AI startups or existing companies using AI to contest the insurers’ pushback. And so, like, it just feels like it’s just going to be bots like— The only money makers are going to be chip companies in Korea because they’re—everyone’s going to be having to get more powerful chips, and old boomer landlords in San Francisco because the employees of OpenAI and Anthropic need a place to live. David Rubenstein - Kenneth Griffin - Michael O’Leary - Robyn Grew | Panel discussion

Norges Bank Investment Management(115.0K) ·26:49 ·14.9K views · 2026-05-16
Kenneth Griffin (Founder and CEO, Citadel) David Rubenstein (Co-Founder, The Carlyle Group) Michael O'Leary (CEO, Ryanair)
This panel of industry leaders—Kenneth Griffin (Citadel), Michael O’Leary (Ryanair), and Robyn Grew (Man Group)—discusses the importance of operational discipline, the inevitable return of the global business cycle, and the critical role of talent an...
TL;DR This panel of industry leaders—Kenneth Griffin (Citadel), Michael O’Leary (Ryanair), and Robyn Grew (Man Group)—discusses the importance of operational discipline, the inevitable return of the global business cycle, and the critical role of talent and "zealous focus" in...

TL;DR

This panel of industry leaders—Kenneth Griffin (Citadel), Michael O’Leary (Ryanair), and Robyn Grew (Man Group)—discusses the importance of operational discipline, the inevitable return of the global business cycle, and the critical role of talent and "zealous focus" in maintaining market leadership.

Operational Strategy and Business Models

  • Citadel’s Dual Structure: Kenneth Griffin (Founder & CEO) differentiates between his two primary entities.
      • Citadel Securities: Operates as a global market maker, facilitating approximately 25% of U.S. equity turnover and averaging $1 trillion in daily volume through advanced predictive analytics.
      • Citadel (Hedge Fund): Shares the same "DNA" of technology and talent but operates with a distinct commercial intent focused on investment returns.
  • Ryanair’s Efficiency Model: Michael O’Leary attributes the airline’s success to a "Formula One pit stop" approach to turnarounds, inspired by Southwest Airlines’ Herb Kelleher.
      • Asset Ownership: Ryanair owns its fleet of 650 Boeing 737s outright to avoid debt and leasing costs.
      • In-house Control: To ensure safety and cost-efficiency, the company performs its own pilot training and maintenance rather than outsourcing.
  • Man Group’s Public Profile: Robyn Grew (CEO) highlights the unique position of being a publicly traded hedge fund.
      • Transparency Advantage: Quarterly reporting provides institutional clients with a level of transparency that builds trust and differentiates the firm from private competitors.

Risk Management and Resilience

  • The 2008 Financial Crisis: Griffin recounts the "tough 16 weeks" in 2008 when Citadel faced potential bankruptcy, noting that history is written by winners and the moment has since become a "footnote" in their success.
  • Legal and Compliance Background: Grew argues that her background as a litigator and compliance officer makes her more effective at risk management.
      • Risk Tolerance: She identifies as having a high risk tolerance, asserting that the best risk managers are those who also understand the necessity of taking risks.
  • Aviation Safety: O’Leary maintains that safety is the bedrock of the business. He notes that while low-cost models are often scrutinized, Ryanair’s 35-year record and youngest fleet in Europe serve as its primary defense against skepticism.

Macroeconomics and Global Fiscal Health

  • Inevitable Recession: Griffin warns that the "business cycle hasn't been banned" and a recession is foreseeable.
      • Fiscal Space: His primary concern is that Western governments are losing the "fiscal space" for counter-cyclical spending due to high sovereign debt.
      • U.S. Policy: Griffin criticizes "socialist" policy leanings in the U.S. and emphasizes that New York and the broader country must put their "fiscal houses in order" to remain pro-business.
  • European Market Dynamics: O’Leary prefers the European market due to deregulation, contrasting it with the "incompetent" government-owned competition like Lufthansa and Air France. He avoids the U.S. market to maintain a "zealous focus" and avoid management distraction.

Talent Acquisition and Leadership

  • Hiring Criteria:
      • Citadel: Griffin seeks a mix of "all-American athletes" with leadership charisma and "wickedly smart" introverts capable of solving complex quantitative problems.
      • Ryanair: O’Leary prioritizes a strong work ethic over academic pedigree, often favoring candidates who worked summer jobs or grew up on farms over those with elite degrees.
  • Gender Diversity in Finance: Grew notes that while Man Group is working to increase female representation, the "gendered nature" of the recruitment pool remains a challenge. She emphasizes that the firm will not "click its fingers" for diversity but remains focused on hiring the best talent.
  • Publicity as a Tool: O’Leary admits his "brash" public persona was originally a necessity driven by a lack of advertising budget, using "cheap PR" to build the brand.
"The Western world has to put its fiscal house in order so that we have more flexibility and more capacity in the next downturn to keep people employed and keep our economies as strong as possible."

— Kenneth Griffin, Founder and CEO of Citadel

"I saw Southwest... Aircraft in, everybody off it, aircraft out, gone. If you could turn planes around in 15 or 20 minutes instead of an hour and 15, you’d get three more free flights per day per aircraft. That’s where it all came from."

— Michael O’Leary, CEO of Ryanair

"I understand risk, I’ve grown up in risk, I’ve run towards risks when sensible people have run away from them. And that makes my understanding of risk management... quite acute."

— Robyn Grew, CEO of Man Group

So Robyn, you are the CEO of Man Group, right? Correct. And you're not a man, right? Since I last looked, yes. And you're the first woman to be the CEO of Man Group in 250 years or something like that? Something like that, yeah. So how did you get to that job? Yeah, determination. How did I get to that job? One, I worked there for a decent amount of time. Two, I've held lots of different roles, and I've got lots of war wounds to show for it. I was the best person for the job when it came open. Okay. And the best person for the job always gets the job? I'd like to think so. Okay. And Ken, your firm has been extraordinarily successful in everything it's touched. But in the Great Recession of 07-08, you came very close to not being that successful. Were you ever worried that you weren't going to survive in the 07-08 period? So 07, no. 07 was actually a phenomenal year for us. But there was a day in 08. I remember getting in the elevator at home and saying, like literally, I hope when I come back, we're still in business. CNBC had a van parked outside of our four walls trying to get that bankruptcy footage. So it was a tough 16 weeks in 2008. Now, the good news is history's written by the winners. We've had a great run since 2008, and that moment's largely become a footnote in our history, but one that I'll never forget. Michael, you're identified as the CEO for a long time of Ryanair, but you didn't start Ryanair. No, Mr. Ryan started it. Had you not been working for him, do you think you would have ever wound up in some capacity like your job you have now? Because the personality you have now is a personality—did you have that personality before or did you grow it as you kind of were running this airline? The question was did I always have a deformed personality? Probably. I was educated by Christian Brothers and then Jesuits, so it was a miracle I survived that. Would I be in the airline industry? No. I would hopefully have gotten a job in a proper functioning industry where you could make money like Kenny. But unlike Robyn, in my case, I got the job in Ryanair because nobody else wanted it. The Ryans had set up Ryanair to be at the start of EU deregulation back in 1987. They wanted to kind of compete with Aer Lingus on the Irish-UK routes and they lost 20 million in three years and the thing was going bust. I was this bright young guy coming out of college, had my accountancy degree. I was actually very quiet, reticent. I never wanted the publicity. Eventually they ran out of chief executives; there was nobody left to run the bloody place. So I was sent in to just staunch the losses, do whatever you want, and go wherever you want to go. My first recommendation was close this turkey, it's never going to make money, it's doomed to bloody fail, this industry is for the birds. And Tony, thankfully, sent me off to see Southwest in the States at the time when Herb Kelleher was doing what he did so well in Austin in Texas. He was a legend and he was the guy who kind of created the low-fare industry, and I was sent off to see him. I have this legendary dinner with Herb Kelleher. What do you remember at the dinner with Herb Kelleher? Absolutely bloody nothing. I was—he was at the time about 65, I was about 26. He drank me under the table before the starters had even got there. But I saw Southwest. I'd come from Europe, where everything was slow. A plane would arrive in, an hour and 15 minutes later turn around because the pilots had to have a smoke and the cabin crew had to have a whinge. Everybody had to call their union to see whether they were feeling like going out in the next five to ten days. This thing arrived in, it was like a Formula One pit stop. Aircraft in, everybody off it, aircraft out, gone. It was pretty clear if you could turn planes around in 15 or 20 minutes instead of an hour and 15 minutes, you'd get two more, three more free flights per day per aircraft. So that's where it all came from. It was a combination of Tony Ryan's stupidity in setting up an airline, but Herb Kelleher's vision in saying you can actually run a very efficient airline, which is the key to Ryanair's success. Now, earlier you mentioned a couple times safety as being an exception to some things. Sometimes people would say, well, if it's a low-cost airline, maybe they're going to scrimp on safety. How can you assure people you're not scrimping on safety? You can't. But ultimately we've now been doing this for about 35 years. We have the youngest, newest fleet of aircraft in Europe. We have a fleet of 650 Boeing 737s. We bought them all from new. We own them all. They're not owned by some bloody leasing company. Depreciation's the only charge. We have no debt on the balance sheet. We train all our own pilots. We have 16 simulator centers. We do all our own aircraft maintenance in-house and we're about to take the engine maintenance in-house as well. So the best you can do is you point to your record over 35 years. Certainly in our first 15 or 20 years it was, "Oh this is a bit cheap and you know, they don't fucking do the maintenance." I think that's kind of disappeared. But you're only as good as today. We do 3,500 flights today; we'll carry 700,000 people today. Today we have to do everything safely, tomorrow we have to do everything safely. It always makes me worry statistically. The bigger and bigger and bigger we get, I always worry statistically you're likely to have a screw-up somewhere along the way. But so far for 35 years we've managed to avoid it. Okay. Ken, many people don't realize you operate two big businesses. One is the most profitable hedge fund ever, Citadel. But you have a separate business, Citadel Securities. What is Citadel Securities and why did you start that business when you already had a very profitable hedge fund? So Citadel Securities is one of the largest market makers in the world. In fact, we trade about 25% of the US equity turnover every day. Over the course of the last couple weeks, we've averaged about a trillion dollars a day in turnover. It is a firm that is at the center point of liquidity around the world. It is powered by world-class technology, great predictive analytics, and a really, really brilliant management team. So it shares much of the same DNA as the hedge fund side of the house. Great people, great technology, great analytics. It's very much similar to what we do in the hedge fund in terms of the culture and ethos, but we do it with a very different commercial intent. Now, there's a story that might be apocryphal. Many stories about people who are famous have apocryphal background stories. One of the apocryphal background stories about you is that when you were in college, you got Harvard University to allow you to set up an antenna that would receive stock quotes so as a student you could trade stock. Is that true? So it was a satellite dish, actually. You know, I was updating my Xbox with my son next to me and he was about 13, he's 18 now. He looks at me and goes, "Dad, you were alive before the internet." And back in the 80s, there was no internet. Stock quotes were actually hard to get. So I had a satellite dish on top of the dorm room. The cable actually came through the fourth-floor window, down an unused elevator shaft and into my room. And that gave me access to real-time prices from the floor of the New York Stock Exchange. And it worked. It worked. And if you hadn't done that or if your early trades hadn't worked, would you be doing something more important like being a lawyer? I'd be doing private equity, David. Right. Okay. Well, there's no higher calling than private equity, of course. Shouldn't you be chasing girls around the college dorm? No. Deferred gratification, Michael. So Robyn, can you explain—you're a publicly traded hedge fund. Yeah. Most hedge funds are privately owned. Why are you publicly owned and what's the advantage of being publicly owned in the hedge fund world? Well, not my choice, by the way. So I haven't sat in that seat. We have roughly 35 years of listing. Advantages: everybody knows what's going on at Man Group. There is nothing to hide. And so in those quarterly updates that we do that Michael looks so forward to doing, we are completely transparent. And clients like that. Institutionally, if you're going to be in a space where people are looking at you and saying, "Hang on a second, are these people who we want to do business with?" sometimes there's an advantage to having to tell everybody what's going on in your firm every quarter. So that's an advantage. Alright. But you came to this firm largely as a lawyer and as a compliance officer. Yep. Usually compliance officers are people who tell you, "You can't do this, you can't do that." How did you—Didn't say I was a good compliance officer. People are telling you you can't do this, you can't do that; usually you don't rise up to be the CEO. So were you a—not that good a compliance officer or—Possibly that. I believe that sometimes the best people to understand risk are the people who want to take the risk as well. If you sit with my executive team, they'll probably sit down and tell you, perhaps the person with the highest risk tolerance in the organization is me. And that's something that either I've grown up on because I was a sort of a litigator, I guess in American terms, or a barrister if you go back into the UK. So I'm an advocate. I understand risk, I've grown up in risk, I've run towards risks when sensible people have run away from them. And that makes my understanding of risk management, which is what we do all day and what we deliver all day, quite acute. So no, it's not usual. Was I a terrible compliance officer? I'm not going to—I don't think I was terrible, but I understand risk. Okay. Michael, not long ago, you said that you wanted to charge people to go to the bathroom in your planes. Did that plan ever work or—and why were people so upset about that, because couldn't they go for 45 minutes without going to the bathroom if they didn't want to pay? This is one of the great free stories that keeps on giving, but I did an interview with some BBC journalist about 25 years ago. I don't know what we were on about, something else. And then I—I'm taking seats out and putting standing cabins, you know like the tube in London, they'd be hanging on to the rails. And she said, "What's next, are you going to charge for toilets?" And I went, "Yeah, we have a team working on that at the moment and you know, if we could take out the two toilets at the back we could put in six seats and six seats with 4% lower fares, then da-da-da." It still comes back every 20- 25- "You're the guy who wants to charge for toilets on board planes." There is actually—we won't do it, but there is a theory in it. Actually if you could take out two of the loos, only have one toilet left and charge for the planes. You look at the extent to which people obsess about not paying for our baggage fees on board. You've people packing up and weighing their bags down on the weigh scales in their bathrooms at home, "I'm not going to pay bloody Ryanair for the bag." They'd do the same thing with the toilet as well on board, and we could put extra seats on the plane and with extra seats we'd be able to charge lower airfares. But we're not going to do it. You talked once about charging extra for people overweight? No, no. We were very good. We actually never did that one. One because it wouldn't make—Don't give him ideas. Don't mind. That was actually—that came out of the States, I think, where with respect, quite a lot of physically challenged people. The Europeans are a bit slimmer, although yes, it's because we're paying so much stupid environmental taxation over here. No, we never planned to charge—aircraft weights are done on average weights for adults and children, so it wouldn't make any difference anyway. But you have a good business in Europe. Why don't you just take that business to the United States? Why don't you go to the United States? Because that is where the culture starts to go wrong, you believe in your own bullshit. This—we work this very well in Europe because we have pretty much incompetent competition in Europe. Lufthansa, Air France, KLM, these guys are run by incompetent European government-owned airlines. The US airlines are a bit more aggressive. They are much stronger and much more well-embedded. And the minute we and our management team says, "This works great in Europe, let's all go off to America, California or Florida, and we can run an airline over there," they'll all be out the door to go to sunshine. I mean, if you've spent any winter in Ireland, everybody wants to get the hell out of the place. So that would be distraction. The other—and I think Kenny's demonstrated over many years in Citadel, zealous focus on your business. Do not get distracted. I don't want to go to—Europe is a deregulated market; the only good thing about Europe is it's a deregulated market for air travel. So thankfully I get very little government interference. The minute I go outside of Europe to the Middle East, to Asia, to the US, it is hugely government-regulated. The States is already a reasonably well-run, although it's quite expensive, air industry. It wouldn't work because—and it would undermine Ryanair because we'd all get distracted. Now, you're trained as an accountant. Chris Whalen: Why Double-Digit Inflation Is Possible, 30-Year Tops 5%

The Julia La Roche Show(72.0K) ·32:43 ·16.6K views · 2026-05-16
Chris Whalen (Chairman, Whalen Global Advisors) Julia La Roche (Host, The Julia La Roche Show) Kevin Warsh (Chair, Federal Reserve)
Chris Whalen warns that double-digit inflation is possible by year-end due to energy shortages and Middle East conflict, while the Federal Reserve prepares for a tectonic shift under Kevin Warsh toward a supply-side, market-driven policy regime.
TL;DR Chris Whalen warns that double-digit inflation is possible by year-end due to energy shortages and Middle East conflict, while the Federal Reserve prepares for a tectonic shift under Kevin Warsh toward a supply-side, market-driven policy regime. Shift in Leadership Style:...

TL;DR

Chris Whalen warns that double-digit inflation is possible by year-end due to energy shortages and Middle East conflict, while the Federal Reserve prepares for a tectonic shift under Kevin Warsh toward a supply-side, market-driven policy regime.

Federal Reserve Overhaul Under Kevin Warsh

  • Shift in Leadership Style: Whalen expects Warsh to break the "model culture" of the Fed, where economists focus on theoretical models over real-world market factors.
  • Balance Sheet Strategy: Reducing Reserves: Warsh will likely push for a smaller balance sheet to return to a pre-2008 regime of "scarce reserves."
      • Policy Trade-off: He may use the tightening effect of a smaller balance sheet as leverage to justify cutting short-term interest rates.
  • Ideological Pivot: Whalen describes the current Fed as a "socialist, statist institution" and views Warsh as a Kemp-style supply-sider who will stop "dumping liquidity into the street" and force banks to find productive uses for capital rather than parking it at the Fed.

Inflation Risks and Energy Shortages

  • Double-Digit Projections: Whalen predicts inflation could hit double digits this year, driven by the war with Iran and its impact on fuel costs.
  • Refining and Rationing: Supply Chain Vulnerability: The world has optimized for low-cost production in the Persian Gulf; disruptions there cannot be fixed overnight.
      • Key Shortages: Potential shortages in high-end lubricants, jet fuel, and synthetic oils could force the Trump administration to consider rationing strategies.
  • Domestic Investment: Fixing these shortages would require significant, long-term investments in U.S. refining capacity and a halt to light crude exports.

Bond Market and Interest Rates

  • The 30-Year Bond: The long bond has topped 5% for the first time since the global financial crisis, reflecting rising inflation fears.
  • Higher for Longer: Even if the Fed drops short-term rates, the "long end" of the curve is likely to remain high—potentially reaching 6%—as markets price in persistent inflation.
  • Treasury Dynamics: Whalen characterizes the Fed as the "tail" and the Treasury as the "dog," noting that massive Treasury issuance (e.g., $800 billion every few months) is the true driver of market liquidity.

Market Trends: AI, Silver, and Real Estate

  • The AI Bubble: Chasing Spend: Investors are chasing the massive capital expenditure in AI rather than profitability. This is fueling a "reflexive" market where passive ETFs pull stock prices higher regardless of fundamentals.
  • Silver as a Tech Trade: Industrial Demand: Unlike gold, silver is currently a commodity and technology story.
      • Drivers: Heavy Chinese buying and the development of solid-state batteries (which require more silver than lithium batteries) are driving prices higher.
  • Real Estate Inequality: Whalen notes a "K-shaped" recovery where asset holders benefit from inflation while workers face an affordability crisis. In "Blue States" like New York, a lack of new construction is keeping prices artificially high despite rising rates.

Mortgage Industry Consolidation

  • Non-Bank Distress: Rising rates are creating a crisis for non-bank lenders who bet on 5% mortgages.
  • United Wholesale Mortgage (UWM): Aggressive Tactics: Whalen compares UWM to Countrywide, noting they are paying "too much" for loans in the secondary market to maintain market share.
  • Industry Outlook: With mortgage rates likely stuck in the 6-7% range, Whalen expects "brutal" downsizing and massive consolidation among mortgage firms that failed to tool down after the Covid-era boom.

Well, first, you got to remember that since the 1930s, the chairman of the Fed in Washington DC has been the chief executive officer of the agency. He does not share authority with the other governors. So he can make changes anyway he wants. You may recall years ago—this was probably before you were born—Alan Greenspan got annoyed at a governor and he just stopped giving him the briefing package. OpenAI's Sam Altman trusts AI will treat most diseases by 2035

CNN(19.6M) ·23:41 ·32.8K views · 2026-05-16
Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI) Kara Swisher (Host)
Sam Altman predicts that AI will be able to treat or mitigate most diseases by 2035, viewing cellular reprogramming and AI-driven discovery as the "Gordian knot" that will solve the root causes of aging and healthcare inequality.
TL;DR Sam Altman predicts that AI will be able to treat or mitigate most diseases by 2035, viewing cellular reprogramming and AI-driven discovery as the "Gordian knot" that will solve the root causes of aging and healthcare inequality. Disease Mitigation by 2035: Altman...

TL;DR

Sam Altman predicts that AI will be able to treat or mitigate most diseases by 2035, viewing cellular reprogramming and AI-driven discovery as the "Gordian knot" that will solve the root causes of aging and healthcare inequality.

AI’s Evolving Role in Healthcare and Longevity

  • Disease Mitigation by 2035: Altman anticipates that within a decade, AI will have the capacity to treat or mitigate the vast majority of human diseases by speeding up the discovery of new biological insights.
  • The "Gordian Knot" of Aging: Most chronic illnesses are "diseases of age." By focusing on longevity, researchers can address the root cause of health degradation rather than fighting individual diseases sequentially.
  • GPT-5 Development: Healthcare is currently a top-three use case for ChatGPT. OpenAI is specifically optimizing GPT-5 to handle complex medical queries, record interpretation, and diagnostic support.
  • Democratizing Elite Care: The long-term vision is a world where every individual has access to medical advice and personalized care that is superior to what the wealthiest people on earth receive today.
      • Diagnostic Tools: Using AI to monitor sleep data, blood tests, and symptoms to provide early warnings for specialists.
      • Personalized Medicine: AI can identify patterns in massive datasets that human doctors might miss, such as specific demographic trends in rare conditions.

Investment in "Retro" and Cellular Science

  • Partial Reprogramming: Altman is a major investor in Retro, a startup focused on "rewinding" cellular age.
      • The goal is to make cells younger without reverting them all the way back to stem cells (which would cause tissue loss).
  • Healthspan vs. Immortality: The objective is to add roughly 10 years of "healthspan"—ensuring people remain healthy and functional longer—rather than achieving literal immortality or "living forever."
  • Startup over Academia: Altman notes a shift in confidence from academia to startups for the implementation phase of science. While academia is good for early discovery, startups are better equipped to turn "crazy exciting" lab discoveries into scalable human treatments.

Risks and the "Human Element" in Medicine

  • Mental Health Concerns: Altman’s primary worry is the speed at which AI is moving into "life coaching" and "therapy" roles. He expresses concern over how rapid societal-scale behavior changes might impact global mental health.
  • Human-Centric Care: Despite AI’s efficacy, Altman believes patients will always prefer human doctors for serious life-and-death situations.
      • The "Prednisone" Threshold: Patients may be comfortable with an AI prescribing medication for a simple rash (like poison oak) but will demand a human connection for a cancer diagnosis.
  • Medical Community Adoption: Contrary to fears of professional rejection, Altman claims many doctors are already using AI "at home" for their work and are requesting HIPAA-compliant versions for professional use.

The Future of AGI and Governance

  • Government Abdication: Altman argues that in a "well-functioning society," AI, longevity research, and fusion energy would be government-led efforts. He believes the private sector has had to move into these spaces because the government abdicated its responsibility for deep scientific research.
  • AGI as a Tool, Not a Creature: Altman rejects the sci-fi trope of self-aware AI "creatures." He views AGI as an incredibly powerful tool that still requires a human "prompt" or goal to accomplish anything.
  • Capitalism and Accessibility: While sci-fi focuses on the rich hoarding technology, Altman believes the "mass market" nature of aging—everyone gets old—will utilize capitalism to make longevity treatments cheap and widely available.

Notable Quotes

"If we could truly keep you, health-wise younger for much longer, that would address a lot of the health issues. And so rather than like kind of try to like fight disease after disease and problem after problem, maybe this was like the Gordian knot we could cut through all of them." — Sam Altman
"I think the prompt that most people want some version of is: help me flourish in accordance with my goals... flourish mentally, physically, everything." — Sam Altman
"I think of it as this tool... it is not going to be this self-aware thing from the sci-fi movies. Even if it goes and does crazy complex things, some person still had to ask the system to go accomplish some goal." — Sam Altman

Figma CEO Dylan Field on Strong Earnings | Bloomberg Tech

Dylan Field (CEO, Figma)
The technology sector is navigating a volatile period defined by high-stakes U.S.-China chip diplomacy, massive capital expenditures by hyperscalers that are beginning to show revenue backlogs, and emerging friction between key AI partners like Apple and OpenAI.
TL;DR The technology sector is navigating a volatile period defined by high-stakes U.S.-China chip diplomacy, massive capital expenditures by hyperscalers that are beginning to show revenue backlogs, and emerging friction between key AI partners like Apple and OpenAI. NVIDIA...

TL;DR

The technology sector is navigating a volatile period defined by high-stakes U.S.-China chip diplomacy, massive capital expenditures by hyperscalers that are beginning to show revenue backlogs, and emerging friction between key AI partners like Apple and OpenAI.

Geopolitics and the Semiconductor Supply Chain

  • NVIDIA and China: President Trump confirmed discussing NVIDIA’s H200 chips with President Xi Jinping during his visit to Beijing.
      • While China expressed a strong desire for the chips, Trump noted that China is simultaneously focused on developing its own domestic semiconductor capabilities.
  • Taiwan Tensions: Chinese state media identified Taiwan as the "number one issue" in U.S.-China relations.
      • President Trump remained non-committal regarding future $14 billion weapon sales to Taiwan, emphasizing a desire for "cooled down" tensions.
  • Workforce Bottleneck: The U.S. semiconductor industry faces a talent gap of approximately 150,000 workers needed to run domestic fabs.
      • Chips Act Funding: Shari Liss (VP, SEMI) highlighted a $200 million investment through the National Science Foundation to build a national workforce development infrastructure.
      • The "Image" Challenge: A primary obstacle is student awareness; many do not associate everyday electronics with the semiconductor manufacturing career path.

AI Infrastructure and Market Performance

  • Cerebras IPO: The chipmaker saw a massive 68% jump in its trading debut, benefiting early venture backers Benchmark, Eclipse, and Foundation Capital.
      • M&A Interest: Reports surfaced that Arm and SoftBank attempted to acquire Cerebras in the weeks leading up to the listing, but were rebuffed.
  • Hyperscaler CAPEX: Alphabet, Meta, and Amazon continue aggressive spending, but investors are finding confidence in a combined $900 billion revenue backlog for Google and Amazon’s cloud divisions.
  • Custom Silicon: Proprietary chips (like Google’s TPUs) are increasingly competitive. While they may not beat NVIDIA GPUs on absolute performance, they offer superior price-to-performance ratios and better margins for the providers.
  • Figma Outlook: Following its earnings report, Figma provided a revenue outlook that topped estimates, citing early traction from charging for new AI-integrated design tools.

OpenAI: Capital Needs and Fraying Partnerships

  • Fundraising Plans: OpenAI CFO Sarah Friar indicated the startup may raise more capital despite its recent multibillion-dollar round, citing the insatiable demand for compute.
  • Apple Friction: The partnership between Apple and OpenAI is reportedly fraying. OpenAI is considering legal action, alleging that the integration into Apple Intelligence is too limited and has failed to produce the expected multi-billion-dollar subscriber growth.
  • Internal Dynamics: Friar described a "healthy tension" between herself and CEO Sam Altman, noting that while they work closely on compute strategy, a CFO must occasionally provide a counter-steer to the CEO.

The Humanoid Robotics Frontier

  • Full-Stack Integration: Brett Adcock (CEO, Figure AI) emphasized that achieving "iRobot-level" capabilities requires vertical integration—designing all motors, sensors, actuators, and AI neural nets in-house.
  • Human Parity: Figure AI’s robots have achieved human-level speed in logistics tasks, operating at roughly one package every three seconds.
  • Operational Shift: The company is moving from 8-hour testing windows to 24/7 autonomous operations to prove the reliability of humanoid systems in commercial environments.

***

Notable Quotes

"China needs it [the H200 chip]. And so, yeah, it came up... but ultimately China wants to develop their own, and that's why we haven't seen any purchases go through." — Donald Trump, President of the United States
"Custom silicon or TPUs from Alphabet and Amazon continues to be one of the most underappreciated narratives in the market... they garner more of the margin by doing it." — Eric Sheridan, Goldman Sachs TMT Group Leader
"To really do this right, like if we want to really build 'iRobot' like the movie... you basically have to design the entire hardware system yourself. This is a full end-to-end vertically integrated system." — Brett Adcock, CEO of Figure AI
"If you want your CEO and CFO to be always agreeing on absolutely everything, you're getting the wrong steer here. That shouldn't be how it works." — Sarah Friar, CFO of OpenAI
